BlackRock CEO on if US-Iran conflict could derail economy as gas prices surge

Fox News(15.1M) ·10:03 ·43.3K views · 2026-03-12
Larry Fink (CEO, BlackRock)
BlackRock CEO Larry Fink views current geopolitical volatility as a long-term "buying opportunity," asserting that the U.S.
TL;DR BlackRock CEO Larry Fink views current geopolitical volatility as a long-term "buying opportunity," asserting that the U.S. economy is entering a transformational era driven by a $10 trillion infrastructure need and AI-led productivity, which necessitates a massive...

TL;DR

BlackRock CEO Larry Fink views current geopolitical volatility as a long-term "buying opportunity," asserting that the U.S. economy is entering a transformational era driven by a $10 trillion infrastructure need and AI-led productivity, which necessitates a massive reinvestment in skilled domestic labor.

Geopolitics and Energy Market Outlook

  • Market Volatility: Larry Fink characterizes the current uncertainty surrounding Iran as short-term noise. He advises investors to ignore immediate volatility in favor of long-dated trends, suggesting that current market dips are a "buy" opportunity.
  • Oil Price Projections: While prices have spiked recently, Fink predicts a potential reversion to lower prices.
      • If the conflict leads to a "neutralized" Iran that returns to the global market, oil could drop below $50 per barrel.
  • Economic Resilience: Fink notes that despite tariffs and geopolitical tension, the market has adjusted rapidly. He credits AI-driven deflationary trends for offsetting the inflationary pressures typically caused by import costs.

The $10 Trillion AI Infrastructure Gap

  • Transformational Technology: Fink compares the impact of AI to the introduction of the steam engine in the 19th century, predicting it will reshape the global economy and radically increase productivity.
  • Investment Requirements: BlackRock estimates a need for $10 trillion in U.S. infrastructure investment over the next eight years to support the AI transition.
      • Grid and Data Centers: Massive capital is required to build out the electrical grid, data centers, and ensure energy resilience.
      • Job Creation: While AI may eliminate some roles, it is expected to create high-paying jobs ($100k–$150k) in construction and technical trades.

The Skilled Labor Crisis and "Future Builders"

  • Demographic Deficit: The U.S. workforce faces a "clear and present" crisis in skilled trades; currently, for every five workers who retire, only two enter the field.
  • Future Builders Initiative: The BlackRock Foundation is launching a $100 million, five-year program to address this gap.
      • Goal: To train 50,000 Americans in essential trades like plumbing and electrical work.
      • Strategic Necessity: Fink argues that if the U.S. does not solve its labor shortage and win the infrastructure race, China will dominate the global technological landscape.

Strategic Competition and the Shift to Pragmatism

  • US-China Relations: Fink views China as both a critical trade partner (specifically for U.S. agriculture) and a primary adversary.
      • Tech Dispersion: He argues the stakes of the rivalry are "broader than winning" at home; the goal is ensuring U.S. allies use American technology rather than Chinese alternatives.
  • Fiduciary Pragmatism: Addressing criticisms of "woke" investing and ESG, Fink states the "pendulum" swung too far five years ago and is now returning to a more pragmatic center.
      • Energy Mix: He emphasizes a "fiduciary-first" approach, investing in both hydrocarbons (e.g., carbon capture with Occidental Petroleum) and renewables (solar and nuclear) based on client demand.
      • Global Trends: He notes that even China is aggressively pursuing a "mixed" energy strategy, leading the world in both nuclear/solar build-out and gas/oil imports.
"We estimate there's going to be a need for 10 trillion dollars of investing in the United States alone over the course of the next eight years... We don't have enough skilled workers to meet the needs of these jobs. We're not ready for it and we have to win. If we don't win this, China's going to win." — Larry Fink
"I don't pay much attention to this short-term volatility, but I focus more on these longer-term trends... I've been getting so many texts: 'What should I do?' And I said, 'Buy more here,' because this is a good long-term opportunity." — Larry Fink
"Do I believe the pendulum five years ago was too far [on ESG]? Yes. I personally am more pragmatic, too... today, because of AI and the overwhelming need for power and electricity, it can't just be one way right now. It can't just be hydrocarbons." — Larry Fink
The workforce is retiring, especially in the skilled trades. This year, for every five who leave, two come in. The arithmetic's not on our side. The perceptions are not on our side. The need is clear and present, and maybe existential. And that's why it's really exciting to see the grown-ups come together and say, "Alright, it's time. This is the year we close the gap." Let's talk about the US economy tonight. Larry Fink is the chairman and CEO of BlackRock, considered the world's largest asset manager. Larry, thanks for being here. Bret, it's great to be here. I want to talk about this new project, the Future Builders Initiative, and the specifics there, but I want to ask you first about how you think this war in Iran, the action in Iran, is affecting investing. Obviously, the global oil market has taken a spike. There is a sense from the president that that's going to be short-lived. How do you look at it from your seat? Well, it's great to be here. Obviously, it creates uncertainty, and uncertainty creates fear. But that being said, the 14.5 trillion dollars of money we manage, most of it is very long-dated. I don't pay much attention to this short-term volatility, but I focus more on these longer-term trends. Do I believe the war is going to be lasting a long time? No. Do I believe oil's going to be reverting back to where it was? Maybe even lower. I mean, if the outcome of the war is a neutralized Iran and they are allowed to be, you know, selling products into the market again—oil products into the market again—I mean, there's probably a great probability that oil is going to be below 50. But once again, that's a short-term move and all that. I do believe the markets find a way to balance out, and the markets are a great efficient mechanism to talk about and identify these short-term issues. We've seen many people pulling out of the market and to me that's the wrong outcome. In fact, I've been getting so many texts: "What should I do?" And I said, "Buy more here," because this is a good long-term opportunity. Was it similar on uncertainty when it came to tariffs and then the Supreme Court ruling and how that's affected things as well, or is it a totally different ballgame? That took almost, you know, close to nine months from the time we had Liberation Day to maybe when the Supreme Court came out with their ruling. I think the market adjusted very rapidly. I mean, we did not see the inflation that we thought we were going to see because of the tariffs. That being said, companies have absorbed some of the costs. I mean, tariffs are added cost to these importation of these products, so we've probably had some inflationary trends because of tariffs. But at the same time, we're having, you know, very large deflationary trends because of the role of AI and how that's playing into the economy. You mention AI. Obviously, a big concern about AI is jobs and what that looks like. You have this Future Builders Initiative. Tell me about this and what's the purpose. Well, we believe that AI is a transformational technology. We believe it's no different than, you know, when we created the trains in the 19th century and transformed our economy. I think this is another transformational technology that will transform the economy. It's going to reshape how the economy is maneuvering. It's going to change in some areas, it's going to change the role of jobs in some businesses, but in many other areas it's going to create an enormous amount of jobs. Where we believe the huge job growth is going to be is in the build-out of our infrastructure. We estimate there's going to be a need for 10 trillion dollars of investing in the United States alone over the course of the next eight years. 10 trillion dollars. And that money's being invested into our economy. Now, what that means is going to be creating a huge amount of jobs in construction, in building out our grid, in building out data centers, making sure that we have energy resilience. And so it's going to create a huge amount of jobs. We don't have enough skilled workers to meet the needs of these jobs. We're not ready for it and we have to win. If we don't win this, China's going to win. And so to me, this is an imperative. We came out with this belief that BlackRock is going to work with the BlackRock Foundation. The Foundation is starting this project called Future Builders, where, you know, we're going to be providing 100 million dollars over five years to help train young Americans to go into these skills, whether it's in plumbing or electricians, to build this out. We think we could help 50,000 Americans build their careers in this. And these AI jobs are, you know, 100, 150 thousand dollar jobs in these fields and we don't have enough. You know, we did this series on AI and all different perspectives of it and the energy aspect of it. There are some people that are afraid of it. You're not one of those. You feel that it is going to be transformational on the upside. I think it creates incredible change and I think it will have dramatic consequences throughout the world. I think AI is going to be the transformational technology to transform an economy, transform health, transform how we live. Does that mean we're going to have changes in the mix of jobs? Yes. But do we believe over time AI's going to create many new jobs, maybe jobs that we can't even imagine? Can it eliminate some form of jobs? Yes. Are we going to create more productivity through that, meaning we're going to do more with fewer people? Yes. Underlining your conversation in a number of these aspects is China and the competition with China. The president's going to China at the end of the month. We'll be taking the show on the road there. What do you think comes out of that? Is this, considering where the world is with Iran, Venezuela, maybe Cuba... it's a different dynamic. He heads there with a different, you know, leverage perhaps, perhaps not. How do you see it? I think we need to find a way to cooperate with a total realization they're a big competitor to us. We still have hundreds of millions, billions of dollars of trade with China, and they are a huge buyer of our agricultural goods; they're the largest buyer of our soybeans. So there are ways of cooperating where we both can find ways to help each other, but we also have to be very mindful: they want to win and we want to win. And there's only going to be one winner. But importantly, the winner, not only just winning in each country, the stakes are broader than winning in the United States or winning in China. The stakes are: the country that creates the technology needs to be the country that disperses the technology. We want to make sure that if we have the best technology, all our allies are using US technology, not Chinese technology. I mean, let me get you to look in the rearview mirror here. Do you believe the woke era was a failed experiment? The ESG, the DEI, the kind of push for that... when you talk about things now you talk in a practical sense, what we can do, what we can get done. Looking back at that, how do you see it? Society moves, the pendulum moves all the time. In BlackRock's case, you know, we are responsible for managing money for everybody. And even today, as you said, maybe these renewables are less talked about. I can tell you right now we have many investors worldwide who are investing in renewables, trying to emphasize solar and other things like that. We're working with Occidental Petroleum right now to build the best carbon capture factories in Texas. So do I believe the pendulum five years ago was too far? Yes. Do I believe we're more, you know, I personally am more pragmatic, too. And I believe... But do you think BlackRock pushed some companies a little bit further left than you thought? It was never our intention because our job is to be—I gotta be—a fiduciary to everybody who gives us money. Our responsibility, Bret, is to make sure if one of our investors wants to invest 100% in hydrocarbons in Texas, I'm investing 100% of the money in Texas. But if another state fund wants us to invest in, let's say, all green energy, we're going to do that. It's their money. But today, because of AI and the overwhelming need for power and electricity, it can't just be one way right now. It can't just be hydrocarbons. If you see what even what China is doing right now, China is rapidly building more nuclear than any other country in the world. They have the largest solar fields in the world. Yet they're the biggest importer of gas and oil. And so to me, more importantly today, I think society has moved into a better position of having more pragmatism. What you're hearing from me is I'm echoing what we're hearing from our clients. Larry, it's fascinating. Thanks very much for the time. You're welcome. Thanks, Bret. Blackrock Infrastructure Summit: Future Builders | Semafor Media Partner

Semafor(23.4K) ·45:34 ·717 views · 2026-03-12
Larry Fink (CEO, BlackRock) Liz Hoffman (Business and Finance Editor, Semafor)
BlackRock is launching a $100 million "Future Builders" initiative to address a critical skilled labor shortage that threatens U.S.
TL;DR BlackRock is launching a $100 million "Future Builders" initiative to address a critical skilled labor shortage that threatens U.S. infrastructure and global competitiveness. As AI disrupts white-collar professions, leaders emphasize a cultural and economic shift toward...

TL;DR

BlackRock is launching a $100 million "Future Builders" initiative to address a critical skilled labor shortage that threatens U.S. infrastructure and global competitiveness. As AI disrupts white-collar professions, leaders emphasize a cultural and economic shift toward the trades, where high demand and massive infrastructure investments are creating lucrative, non-degree career paths.

The Skilled Labor Crisis and the "Future Builders" Initiative

  • Closing the Gap: The BlackRock Foundation has committed $100 million to a national philanthropic initiative focused on connecting workers to skilled trades training.
      • Market Demand: According to the Bureau of Labor Statistics, hundreds of thousands of net new skilled trade jobs are required to meet current infrastructure demand.
      • The "Work" Gap: There are currently 7.6 million open positions in the U.S., while 6.9 million prime-working-age men are affirmatively not looking for work.
  • The "Shop Class" Consequence: Mike Rowe attributed the current crisis to the removal of vocational training from high schools in the 1970s.
      • Cultural Stigma: The trades were framed as a "vocational consolation prize," leading to a generation that views aspirational careers as exclusively degree-based.
      • Retirement Wave: For every five skilled tradespeople who leave the workforce, only two enter, creating an existential threat to the maritime, energy, and construction industries.

AI Disruption and the Recalibration of Education

  • The Value of Degrees: While Larry Fink acknowledged the social value of college, he noted that tuition has outpaced inflation for 30 years, undermining the value proposition for many students.
      • AI Impact: AI is expected to disrupt white-collar "whiteboard" jobs faster than manual trades, potentially leading to high unemployment among college graduates even in a growing economy.
      • High-Earning Trades: Mike Rowe cited data center electricians under age 30 earning $240,000 to $280,000 annually without college debt, noting they are frequently "poached" by competitors due to scarcity.
  • Corporate Shifts: BlackRock is seeing revenue growth outpace headcount growth as technology allows the firm to "do more with fewer people," a trend expected to accelerate across the corporate sector.

Infrastructure as a National Security Imperative

  • Global Competition: Fink warned that if the U.S. does not develop abundant, affordable power and modern infrastructure, "China wins."
      • Energy Strategy: China is currently building 100 gigawatts of nuclear power and miles of solar fields; the U.S. must accelerate solar, battery storage, and hydrocarbon development to compete.
      • Capital Requirements: An estimated $10 trillion of investable money is needed over the next eight years to rebuild American infrastructure.
  • Onshoring Manufacturing: Fink advocated for a "51% ownership" model—similar to China’s requirements—to bring Korean or Chinese battery and solar manufacturers to the U.S. to create local jobs.

Financial Literacy and the Wealth Gap

  • Broadening Participation: Currently, 40% of Americans have no capacity to invest in the financial markets. Fink argues that the wealth gap is driven by capital appreciating faster than salaries.
      • Retirement Solutions: BlackRock advocates for "childhood accounts" (starting with $1,000) to allow every citizen to benefit from the "technological boom" and long-term market growth.
  • The "Jumpstart Bill": Mike Rowe highlighted proposed legislation (the Jumpstart Act) that would allow 529 plans to be used for "tools of the trade," such as trucks and equipment, rather than just tuition.
  • Economic Outlook: Fink expressed minimal concern regarding long-term inflation, suggesting that if wage growth is coupled with technology-driven productivity, the net result is a "good outcome" for the economy.

Notable Quotes

"Nothing in the history of Western civilization has gotten more expensive more quickly than a four-year degree. It’s not to say it’s not valuable, but... we encouraged kids to borrow whatever it took to take that path... and told them if you don't, you're screwed."

— Mike Rowe, Founder, mikeroweWORKS Foundation

"If we don't rebuild America's infrastructure, if we do not have an America that has abundant power that is affordable, which is the foundation for AI—if we don't have that, China wins."

— Larry Fink, Chairman and CEO, BlackRock

"What the country needs are more philosophers who can run an even bead [weld] and appreciate the magnificence of something built, and more welders who can have a conversation about the Stoics... The color of collars is over."

— Mike Rowe, Founder, mikeroweWORKS Foundation

"I am more bullish on the United States than any other country... but we need more and more Americans investing in this growth or having a piece of the American growth. We don't talk about that enough."

— Larry Fink, Chairman and CEO, BlackRock

Now, please welcome BlackRock's Global Head of Corporate Affairs, John Kelly. Okay, welcome back, folks. Please take a seat. We're about to start our panel with Larry Fink and Mike Rowe. But before we do, as you get settled, I just want to tell you how proud I am of the initiative that we launched today, our Future Builders initiative. Much like everything else we do at BlackRock, we really rely on the expertise of our clients and others in partnerships with government at all levels, and this initiative is really exceptional. In that, you know, we are not—we're experts at a lot of things, but we really rely on you all to tell us how we do this big initiative. And so this is the beginning of our journey. It's about the American worker and how to close the skilled trades gap. This video here will explain a little bit more detail, but we want to thank you. We're at the beginning of this journey. We're grateful for the partnership and we're on our way. So, roll the video. Thank you. For nearly 40 years, BlackRock has helped Americans save and prepare for their future. As we enter a new era of infrastructure development, there is a powerful opportunity to translate this investment into lasting economic opportunity for people and communities across the country. Becoming a master plumber opened so many doors. It was the best decision I ever made. According to the Bureau of Labor Statistics, hundreds of thousands of net new skilled trade jobs will be needed in the coming years to meet demand. That's why The BlackRock Foundation is launching a national $100 million philanthropic initiative to help close the skilled labor gap, connecting people to skilled trades training and putting them on the path to become future builders. With commitments like this, we have the opportunity to train young people faster so that we get them on the job sites and help them start their career down a good path of success for them and their families. This generation of workers has an opportunity none of us ever had prior to this moment in history. By investing in training and access today, we're helping ensure more people can participate in and benefit from the infrastructure transformation underway across the country. Now, please welcome the founder of the mikeroweWORKS Foundation, Mike Rowe, and Chairman and CEO of BlackRock, Larry Fink, moderated by Semafor's Business and Finance Editor, Liz Hoffman. Hi, everyone. Larry, Mike. Hi, Liz. Hi, Liz. Thanks for coming. Larry, you underwrite risk for a living. Is a four-year college degree the most expensive bad investment in America right now? No. Because for many—for me, I had no idea what I wanted to do. I mean, for me, it took me more than four years to decide what I wanted to do. So I was able—you know, I think college is a great moment for socialization, of equalization, of maturity. So much about life is about the human connectivity. And so I do believe there's a great value beyond the education. As I said, I still believed I was quite immature after my four-year degree, so I was able to talk myself into staying on for two more years and getting my MBA without ever working. By that time, I think I was a little more mature to go do it. But let's be clear. For many people, there are other paths. There are other paths to build a wonderful life and a wonderful career. And the key is, you know, we have to look at the essence of each and every person. The key for life for everyone is to find their purpose. For some people, their purpose will remain to get a four-year or an advanced degree and they could take that forward. But that's not going to be the pathway for everybody. And I think in the United States, and this is going to be one of the key critical issues we have, I think the speed at which AI is changing, we're not adapting our society fast enough. You see, and you know, really post-World War II, the pathway to a white-collar job was a university education, a college education. AI is going to disrupt many of those type of jobs. At the same time, AI is going to create a lot of skilled jobs needs. And the biggest issue confronting our country today and other countries is the speed at which this change is occurring. Is society moderating that fast enough? I've had many conversations with university leaders and what are they going to do about it, because there's no question in my mind that demand for four-year degrees is probably going to be going down. And how are we—you know, if you look at the growth rate of tuition in our university system, I think it's now for 30 years in a row growing faster than inflation. This obviously asks the question: is that a good value proposition 30 years later? I mean, I went to school, I was a product of California, where going to a university system was $600 a year—$200 a quarter, and you went to three of them. So, I mean, I was able to have a great value for that education at that $600 a year. That's not the equation today. It's—we're talking hundreds of thousands of dollars for a four-year education in some cases. So, we need to be recalibrating how we are designing our society and getting our society prepared. This is one of the foundational reasons why we thought about providing this philanthropic gift to start Future Builders. Because we need to highlight there's other pathways for dignity and purpose. And with the rise of AI and how AI is going to reshaping us, it's going to create many jobs and we're not prepared as a society to fulfill those jobs. And to me, this is a crisis. Mike, part of this is cultural, that we've steered generations of kids towards, as Larry says, college degrees—four-year degrees, six-year degrees—and that's going to be slow to change. But as you sort of look out, what is the cultural piece of this? We were talking backstage a little bit that young people seem not to be okay, and a lot of that is real uncertainty, growing uncertainty about where they're going to get wages and then where they're going to get kind of a sense of purpose. Yeah, the kids are not alright, you know, not to put too fine a point on it. But nothing in the history of Western civilization has gotten more expensive more quickly than a four-year degree. It's not to say it's not valuable, but I mean nothing—not real estate, not healthcare, not energy, nothing. So, that kind of happened and like a frog in the boiling water, I think a lot of parents watched it happen. And today, people still ask me, how did it get so expensive so fast? And the honest answer is a 300-page book I can't get around to finishing. But I thought and think that it started with a very deliberate decision to get shop class out of high school back in the early '70s. In fact, I would say that in the long history of education, you'd be hard-pressed to find a more boneheaded decision that led to more unintended consequences. We removed from our high schools not just metal shop and woodshop and all those other things, we removed the vocations that actually looked like work. So a kid who maybe wasn't even interested didn't have the chance on his way from math class to English class to poke his head in to see what was being built, what was being fabricated, what miracle was unfolding in that shop. That was just gone. And at the same time, we really put our thumb on the scale, I think. We wanted to encourage more kids to get a higher education, whatever that means. And so, as with most PR, we went too far. We weren't satisfied with making a persuasive case for a four-year degree or beyond; we had to say if you don't take that path, you're screwed. You're going to wind up turning a wrench, you're going to wind up with some vocational consolation prize, right? That message landed. And it scared parents to death. And it gave kids a clear example of what an aspirational career looked like. And it didn't look like work. So you take that over 35 years or so—drip, drip, drip—countless portrayals in modern media about the vocational consolation prize. That was Schneider on *One Day at a Time*, some of you will remember. You can't find a plumber on TV that's not 350 pounds with a giant butt crack, right? All these portrayals, they're alive and well, they've been with us forever, right? That landed, that's powerful. And so, we freed up a bottomless pile of money, we encouraged kids to borrow whatever it took to take that path. Their parents were scared. Their guidance counselors are getting bonused on their ability to get more kids to matriculate toward that direction than the other. You know, how did college get so expensive? How could it not get that expensive? That's what we did. And so, today, there's $1.7 trillion in student loans currently on the books, most of which is not held by trade school graduates. 7.6 million open positions, many of which are in manufacturing and the trades. According to Nick Eberstadt, whose terrific book you should all read, it's called *Men Without Work*, we've got 6.9 million men—prime-working age men—not only not working, but affirmatively not looking for work. That's never happened in this country, not in peacetime, anyway. So, all of these things have conspired to bring us to this place. And by the way, thank you—thank you for doing this. It's—I can't even tell you how gratifying it is for me to have spent the last 17 years talking about our crumbling infrastructure and trying to make a more persuasive case for the jobs that we just saw up there on the screen. If I had an alarm bell, I would ring it. Not a week goes by that I don't hear from the leader of some consequential industry who is freaking out in real time. The maritime industrial base needs 400,000 people to build our submarines. Welders and electricians and CNC operators—400,000. "Where are they?" they said, you know. "We've looked everywhere. Do you know?" And I said, "Yeah, man, they're in the eighth grade." That's where they are. You know, we've talked about AI, we've talked about data centers, we've talked about the incredible opportunities that have come. Dina just mentioned a program I didn't even know about in Ohio laying fiber optics that guarantees you a job. There are so many great stories to tell. This is one more in the page. Sorry for the filibuster, but when we talk about why our workforce is out of balance, I think it makes perfect sense to start with: why do we paint with such a broad brush when it comes to telling kids what the best path for the most people is? That's why we're out of whack today, I believe. And as to why we're still lending money we don't have to kids who are never going to be able to pay it back to train them for a bunch of jobs that are about to become completely irrelevant—yeah, that's beyond my paygrade. We have been assuming that these jobs—these electricians, welders, HVAC, all that stuff—is less vulnerable to AI, in part I think because the videos we see of robots are always like falling down and helpless and taking an hour to unload a dishwasher. But the tech will obviously get better and there are those in Silicon Valley who have a different solution to this problem, which is like a million robots out doing it. And I don't know sort of which—maybe Larry, like what those competing visions are. For a long time we told kids learn to code, turns out that was pretty short-lived advice. How do you future-proof this particular piece of advice that you're giving and the money you're putting behind it? Well, there's no question—I mean, Elon Musk is now saying we're going to have more robots than humans in a number of years. But they're going to do smaller, you know, they're going to do different tasks. There's no question, as Mike was talking about, in so many industries where we are short workers, short finding the talent to take on these jobs. I don't—you know, I don't know where robotics is going to be going. I'm pretty optimistic that they're going to be improving and getting better at what they do. But that being said, I do not believe the large-scale projects that we are talking about in rebuilding America is anything short-term. It's probably much longer term where we're going to have that type of machinery in the future. But that being said, the opportunities for the next 10-plus years is going to be the rebuild of America. Let me be clear. If we don't rebuild America's infrastructure, if we do not have an America that has abundant power that is affordable—which is the foundation for AI—if we don't have that, China wins. I mean, they're building 100 gigawatts of nuclear as we speak. They're putting solar fields that are miles and in length. We need to be moving now and, as Mike talking about, we can't afford to wait until these eighth graders matriculate. We have to start focusing on it now. We need to get—you know, what I'm worried about is this May when our college graduates are graduating, we may see the highest unemployment rate of that group of young men and women in years without a recession. And so we need to be talking about it. We need to be thinking about it as a society. We need to be moving forward. There's other pathways, and hopefully we're all waking up to that idea that, you know, we need to be rebuilding our power grids, we need to be producing as much power as we can with any type of molecule that you could have or any type of— So, I am in Washington even now. I'm pushing that we need to be talking much louder about solar. I mean, solar has to be a component of pragmatic energy policy. And so the combination of the strength of our economy and the geology of our country related to the hydrocarbons, that's a foundation. And that's something that we have that so many other countries don't have. But if you travel around the world like many of us do in business, you're seeing the growth of solar in so many ways because every country needs to solve their power issue. Whether that's in India or Africa or anything else, they don't have the hydrocarbons like that we do. And the advancements in battery storage and the advancements in solar panels is growing so rapidly that solar is going to become a very viable source. Now, what we need to, to get back to more jobs, we need to build those powers supplies, the solar panels, the battery storage platforms here in the United States. We need to be partnering up with companies—whether they're Korean or even Chinese—have 'em come here, build it. We could own 51% of their businesses, like what happens over in China. We could be doing this and rebuild and creating real, real jobs again. But we need to have a plan and a policy. You're talking about, you know, potentially a very high unemployment numbers while the economy is growing, will continue to grow. There's, I think, growing concern about sort of jobless growth and— But Mike said it. We don't have enough skilled workers. There's so many job openings right now. And we're hiring, we have a bunch of young men and women who chose the path of getting a college degree right now where that disruption is occurring very rapidly. Palantir CEO on Iran, AI Weapons and America's Advantage | a16z American Dynamism Summit

a16z(254.0K) ·32:26 ·46.7K views · 2026-03-12
Alex Karp (Cofounder and CEO, Palantir)
America has re-established a decisive technological deterrent against adversaries like China and Russia, but this advantage is fragile; Silicon Valley must embrace its obligation to the warfighter and national defense or risk the eventual nationalization of the technology industry.
TL;DR America has re-established a decisive technological deterrent against adversaries like China and Russia, but this advantage is fragile; Silicon Valley must embrace its obligation to the warfighter and national defense or risk the eventual nationalization of the technology...

TL;DR

America has re-established a decisive technological deterrent against adversaries like China and Russia, but this advantage is fragile; Silicon Valley must embrace its obligation to the warfighter and national defense or risk the eventual nationalization of the technology industry.

The Re-establishment of American Deterrence

  • Technological Dominance: Karp asserts that America has regained a deterrent capability that was previously "eviscerated." This is driven by a hybrid of software, hardware, and AI—evidenced by recent operations where U.S. technology demonstrated total dominance over adversarial systems.
  • The Decisive Vote: In a world of competing powers (U.S., China, Russia), military superiority is the only way for the West to maintain a "decisive vote" on global rules and human rights.
  • The Warfighter Priority: Palantir’s core mission is ensuring American warfighters return home safely while ensuring adversaries do not. Karp argues that those who benefit from American freedom yet refuse to support the military are "effing spoiled."
  • Meritocracy in Defense: The Department of Defense is highlighted as America's most revered and meritocratic institution, serving as a primary vehicle for social mobility across all demographics.

The Silicon Valley Wake-Up Call

  • Risk of Nationalization: Karp warns that if Silicon Valley continues to automate white-collar jobs while simultaneously distancing itself from the military, a "horseshoe effect" will occur where both political wings agree to nationalize the tech industry.
  • Strategic Blindness: He critiques elite tech leaders for having high IQs but low strategic awareness regarding the political consequences of their isolationism.
  • The "Hollywood" Model: Karp suggests the tech industry needs a self-regulatory framework similar to the Hollywood rating system to address ethical concerns (privacy, Fourth Amendment rights) before Washington imposes "butchered" regulations.
  • Bridge Building: There is a fundamental cultural "language barrier" between Silicon Valley and the Department of Defense. Founders must move beyond elite bubbles—visiting military bases and middle-America—to understand the perspectives of those using their technology.

The Zero-Sum Reality of AI

  • Global Competition: While Silicon Valley prefers to view technology as "positive-sum," Karp argues the AI race is strictly zero-sum. If the U.S. does not set the rules through dominance, China or Russia will.
  • Palantir’s Competitive Niche: Karp positions Palantir as the "last company standing" in the AI race because it focuses on the specificity, security, and orchestration (ontology) of data rather than just the underlying Large Language Models (LLMs).
  • Privacy vs. Utility: He defines Palantir as an "anti-surveillance" company, arguing that the protection of Fourth Amendment rights is essential even as technology becomes capable of imputing private behavior.

Cultivating Neurodivergent Talent

  • The American Advantage: Karp identifies America’s single greatest competitive advantage as its ability to protect and augment unconventional, "neurodivergent" talent (e.g., individuals with dyslexia or autism).
  • Individualism as Strategy: The U.S. wins by allowing iconoclastic individuals to follow their own "playbooks" rather than forcing them into bureaucratic "straight jackets."
  • Management Philosophy: Karp views himself as an "artist" who manages high-aptitude outliers by helping them express unique solutions that only they could build, then "anti-dyslexifying" their workflow to ensure those solutions scale within a corporate or military framework.

***

Notable Quotes

"We are the power that actually has the decisive vote. And there is only one way to do that, and that is with military superiority." — Alex Karp
"If Silicon Valley believes we are going to take away everyone's white-collar job... and you're going to screw the military... if you don't think that's going to lead to nationalization of our technology, you're retarded." — Alex Karp
"Our single advantage is to augment neurodivergent, highly individual people to be their absolute unique best and protect their First, Second, Fourth, and Fifth Amendment rights so that they don't get screwed." — Alex Karp

Elon Musk: Optimus 3 Is Coming, Recursive Self-Improvement Is Already Here, and the Singularity #239

Peter H. Diamandis(475.0K) ·24:05 ·223.6K views · 2026-03-12
Elon Musk (CEO, Tesla and SpaceX) Peter H. Diamandis (Founder, XPRIZE and Singularity University)
Elon Musk predicts a "hard takeoff" for AI with full recursive self-improvement by 2025, leading to a 10x expansion of the global economy within a decade and an eventual post-scarcity era of "Universal High Income.
TL;DR Elon Musk predicts a "hard takeoff" for AI with full recursive self-improvement by 2025, leading to a 10x expansion of the global economy within a decade and an eventual post-scarcity era of "Universal High Income." Human-Out-of-the-Loop: Musk states that we are already...

TL;DR

Elon Musk predicts a "hard takeoff" for AI with full recursive self-improvement by 2025, leading to a 10x expansion of the global economy within a decade and an eventual post-scarcity era of "Universal High Income."

AI Evolution and Recursive Self-Improvement

  • Human-Out-of-the-Loop: Musk states that we are already in a "hard takeoff" phase where successive AI models are built by their predecessors. Full automation of model building—recursive self-improvement without humans in the loop—is expected by the end of 2024 or 2025.
  • Grok Performance Benchmarks:
      • Prediction: Musk claims Grok 1.5 is currently the best model for predictive tasks.
      • Coding: While currently trailing competitors, Musk expects Grok to "catch up and exceed" rivals in coding capabilities by mid-2024.
  • The Intelligence Explosion: Future AI intelligence is expected to exceed human intelligence by orders of magnitude that are currently impossible to comprehend. Musk envisions a future where intelligence harnesses a million times more energy than Earth's current total electricity usage.

The Robotics Revolution: Optimus Timeline

  • Production Schedule: The "Optimus 2" humanoid robot is in final development. Production is slated to begin Summer 2024 with a slow initial ramp, reaching high-volume production in 2025.
  • Annual Design Cycles: Musk intends to release a new, improved robot design every year, with "Optimus 3" already planned for a 2025 design phase.
  • Manufacturing Scale:
      • Tesla is building roughly 10 million square feet of factory space specifically for Optimus production.
      • While Tesla employs ~150,000 people, Musk anticipates that automation will make the "output per human" extraordinarily high, though he does not foresee immediate layoffs.

Macroeconomic Projections and "Universal High Income"

  • 10x Economic Growth: Barring World War III, Musk predicts the global economy will be more than 10 times its current size within 10 years.
  • Universal High Income (UHI): Musk suggests the world will move beyond Universal Basic Income (UBI) to "Universal High Income."
      • Deflationary Abundance: Because AI and robots will produce goods and services at a rate far exceeding the money supply, massive deflation will occur, making products essentially "saturated" and widely available.
  • The Irrelevance of Money: Musk envisions a post-capitalist future similar to Ian Banks' *Culture* series, where traditional currency becomes irrelevant and the only meaningful metrics are "wattage and tonnage" (power and mass).

Space Exploration and the Singularity

  • Ten-Year Space Milestones: Musk expects a permanent base on the Moon, the implementation of "mass drivers" (electromagnetic catapults) on the lunar surface, and a human presence on Mars within the next decade.
  • Solar System Intelligence: Musk notes that Earth only intercepts half a billionth of the Sun’s energy. He predicts the majority of future intelligence will eventually exist off-planet to harness the Sun's full energy output.
  • Solving Human Suffering: Musk believes super-intelligence will solve "everything you can possibly think of," specifically citing the elimination of back pain and the extension of human healthspan as primary benefits.

Notable Quotes

"Humans are gradually getting less and less in the loop on recursive self-improvement. Every successive model is built by the one before it... It may be there end of this year, but not later than next year." — Elon Musk
"I'd say the economy is ten times its current size in ten years. Greater than. In the absence of World War III, if current trends continue, I would say the economy 10x's in ten years." — Elon Musk
"AI and robotics is also the only way we're going to solve our budget deficit frankly, and not just go bankrupt as a country." — Elon Musk
"The challenge will be even vaguely appreciating that level of intelligence. But it's safe to say it will solve everything you can possibly think of. Longevity being certainly one of them." — Elon Musk
