Please welcome Principal Military Deputy Assistant Secretary of the US Navy for Research, Development, and Acquisition, Vice Admiral Seiko Okano. Good morning, thanks for having me here. Under Secretary Del Toro's leadership, the Department of the Navy is changing how we do business. We're done waiting for purpose-built government solutions when the best technology in the world's already proven in this room. We need speed, we need scale, and we're going to get this by deploying commercial AI directly into the maritime industrial base. This is what ShipOS is and why we're here together today. What you're about to see on the screen are capabilities of ShipOS, the Department of the Navy's AI-enabled operating system for the naval shipbuilding enterprise. We started deliberately. Two shipbuilders, three public shipyards, and 18 suppliers, all focused on submarine construction and maintenance. That's not the full industrial base that the Navy has; that's just the beachhead. And from that beachhead, the agents are already working, surfacing schedule opportunities, flagging material risk, identifying capacity that can be reallocated before it becomes a gap. Secretary Del Toro has been unambiguous: we measure by outcomes. Does the fleet have what it needs? ShipOS will enable us to answer that question continuously with real-time visibility across shipyards, key suppliers, and production schedules. I want to show you two workflows inside the platform. Now we're inside a supplier's view of their production timeline. Anyone in this room who's worked in manufacturing or defense acquisition knows what an engineering change notice means in practice. It means a phone tree, emails, somebody manually updating the production schedule, calling procurement, tracking down the shop floor supervisor, trying to hold all of it in their head long enough to figure out what breaks. In naval shipbuilding, a single change notice can cascade across dozens of suppliers, hundreds of work orders, and a production timeline measured in years. Watch what happens when the same change notice enters ShipOS. What used to land on somebody's desk as a problem now arrives as a decision, with context, options, and tradeoffs already mapped. Every purchase order downstream of that change is immediately flagged; every production stage affected is visible. The system doesn't wait for a human to map the cascade; it already has. ShipOS opens the change notice and the AIP agent starts running, parsing source documents, cross-referencing the bill of materials, identifying every downstream dependency, simultaneously in parallel. Think of it as a playoff run. Every play calls the next. A missed block in the first quarter doesn't just lose a down, it changes what's available in the fourth. Now here's where the human comes back in. ShipOS presents three paths forward: Act now, minimal schedule and cost impact; Defer, defined cost growth, defined schedule risk; Reject and escalate, full manual review, maximum exposure. Each path shows the tradeoffs clearly: days, dollars, risk score. The program manager sees the full picture, makes the call, and approves. Agents execute, systems update, notifications go out, the production plan is revised. The result: a decision that takes minutes, with a complete audit trail that documents itself. Now I want to show you something that will look very familiar to everybody in this room: an inbox. This is the ShipOS intelligent comms pipeline, a live view of incoming supplier communications automatically triaged as they arrive. Some threads are already being worked by agents, some are flagged for human review, some are already resolved without anyone touching them. Now watch what happens when we open one. A message from a lead mechanic on the shop floor includes two issues: equipment shows signs of wearing, pulling more power than baseline, same pattern as a failure they had had before; and a material shortage, parts needed for work starting Monday that haven't been confirmed yet. 16 workers potentially standing idle if it isn't resolved. ShipOS reads the message; it resolves shop floor shorthand, informal references to locations and equipment, back to the actual assets in the system. It pulls the relevant telemetry, it finds the matching failure patterns in historical maintenance records, it checks inventory, confirms the shortfall, locates an inbound shipment that covers the gap, and stages a preventative maintenance work order. Then it drafts the response. One email, two problems that would typically require separate people, separate systems, and hours of back-and-forth handled in the time it takes to read the message. That means every supplier, every subcontractor, every program manager, regardless of their own digital maturity, can participate in an intelligent, connected workflow simply by doing what they already do: sending emails, sharing documents. ShipOS meets the industrial base where it is and it lifts the entire enterprise. It doesn't ask them to change how they work; it meets them where they are and makes every message they send more actionable than it would ever have been on its own. What you are seeing now is where it all comes together. Every automated change assessment, every intelligently routed communication, every agent-orchestrated action all flowing into a single dynamic production schedule that reflects the true state of the program. A more accurate schedule means risk is identified earlier, intervention happens sooner, schedule recovery is reduced, cost growth is contained. And ultimately it means this: capability to the fleet delivered at speed. Not the software, not the operating system—the ship in the water, the submarine at sea, ready to execute when the nation calls. The Department of the Navy has made a strategic bet. The faster path to a stronger fleet runs directly through a smarter, more agile industrial base. ShipOS is how we turn that decision into results. The next era of naval power will be shaped not only in the fleet but in the shipyards that build it. We've seen how AI has transformed the fight; now it's transforming how we build. ShipOS gets more capability as our partners work with us to deploy it, each optimizing their own operations, maintaining full control of their data and security, and working alongside systems that already have. The Navy has made its commitment and is transforming how it acquires and builds capability for the fleet. Rebuilding the maritime industrial base will require the innovation, partnership, and leadership represented in this room. And that work is already underway. Thank you. Please welcome President and Chief Executive Officer of World View, Ryan Hartman. World View exists to inspire, create, and explore new perspectives for a radically improved future. I know that's very aspirational and so it's my job here today to make that real. So right now above us, high in the atmosphere, is the stratosphere. It's an operable domain. Most of human history has largely ignored the stratosphere; too high for aircraft to loiter, too low for satellites to orbit, just an atmospheric layer that you learned about in school, not well enough understood to operationalize. World View exists to change that. We build stratospheric platforms that can stay over an area for a very long period of time—30 days, 60 days, or more. We do this by leveraging four-directional winds that exist in the stratosphere. And by controlling our altitude with ballasted air, we can freely navigate in the stratosphere. And we can do what we call station keeping. It's simple in principle but complex in execution because you're steering a month-long mission using the physics of the atmosphere. Navigation becomes a vertical decision that creates a horizontal result. Stratospheric sensing delivers imagery that is higher resolution than any satellite with much longer endurance than UAVs. UAVs can get close and they're precise but their endurance is limited to hours. Satellites can be global and predictable but they also have fixed orbits and limited revisits. I'm happy to report that the stratosphere is now open for business. For our customers, this isn't a debate about which domain wins. It's about getting the predictability of a satellite, the precision of a UAV, and the enduring presence of a stratospheric platform, all stitched together in one operational picture. Because when minutes matter, data isn't the product, decisions are. And here's the core idea I want you to take away from us today: the stratosphere is operational, and AI-driven workflows built on Palantir are how we scale it. Let me show you why that matters, beginning with a hard constraint we used to accept. Previously, from initial flight plan to launch it could take us no less than two weeks. Two weeks works for a scientific payload but two weeks is unacceptable for an operational demand that shifts by the hour. So our first focus with Palantir was simple: compress time without compromising safety, compliance, or mission objectives. And that's where our journey with Palantir began. In January, we partnered with Palantir and in a very short period of time we delivered AI Flight Director powered by Palantir's Maverick mission planning. Here's what that means. A stratospheric mission isn't a point-to-point flight; it's a multi-week, sometimes multi-month operation conducted at the edge of Earth's atmosphere, riding winds that don't care about our schedule. Our customers obviously do care. They care about exact mission criteria—things like coordinates, dwell time, sensor coverage, and the ability to adapt when the world changes. So we use AI Flight Director to simulate optimal trajectories that meet customer objectives while operating in a dynamic, sometimes unpredictable environment. And we do it in a way operators can trust. They can audit it and then they can execute it. Now the second piece with Palantir: planning is only half of the battle. I'll tell you the real fight is disruption. A sudden sigmet, a storm cell that accelerates, a wind shift that turns a safe trajectory into a risk. Or an intelligence briefing and the AOI has shifted based on new intelligence. Systems must be immediately retasked to transit to the new location. Historically that becomes a scramble using spreadsheets, computer modeling, phone calls, manual replans, and time that you just do not have. With Palantir, we connect live telemetry from the balloon's sensors and combine that with live meteorological streams and operational constraints, and then we make the operation AI aware in real time. So instead of an operator hunting for the right data, Maverick's mission planning agent surfaces the alert, it contextualizes it, and proposes a set of actions: approve a reroute that an AI Flight Director already modeled, adjust to catch a different wind band, update a constraint and see downstream effects instantaneously. And this is where the stratosphere ceases being a platform that collects data and starts becoming a platform that participates in decisions. And the outcome isn't just speed; it's safety and predictability at scale. At the end of every flight, we take what happened, we take what we learned, all the things that we decided and what resulted, and we write it back. And with that every mission, the ontology becomes a living memory of the operation—past events, decisions, and outcomes enriching every future flight plan and execution. And so that's what we've built so far: an AI Flight Director that compresses time and makes execution adaptive. Now let's talk about what happens next because this is the part that changes the economics. It's easy to celebrate a single launch but the scaling story is completely different. Palantir-powered workflows don't just make one launch faster; they make dozens or a hundred simultaneous launches possible with the same operational efficiency. What they do is operationalize a swarm. And a swarm is not a marketing term; it's a control problem. When you have many assets in flight you need fleet orchestration, automated deconfliction, dynamic retasking, and prioritized attention so humans are focused where judgment is required. And we can now see the path to that system. Imagine this: multiple stratospheric platforms in flight, each streaming sensor data, each running edge inference, each capable of being retasked in minutes. A potential object of interest appears, the platform detects it, the system distills it, and an AI agent contextualizes it. The operator gets a recommendation with the rationale and the action is executed. And that's the future of in-situ decision-making. And it matters because it changes what's strategically possible. We had a roadmap to get there in 2030 and we can now see that possible in 2026. And that's why we're partnering with Ondas. This partnership immediately creates a multi-domain ISR alliance and a suite of offerings that combine World View stratospheric balloon systems with Ondas' expansive portfolio of unmanned systems. This partnership accelerates something we already believe: that the future is multi-domain executed as one coordinated system. And so here's what changes. The same Palantir native workflows that plan and operate stratalites become the backbone for orchestrating multiple domains: stratospheric platforms, UAVs in groups one, two, and three, and space-based assets. Today across the industry, systems have always had their own walled garden: separate data feeds, separate tools, separate teams, separate timelines, and customers end up patching together fragments just to infer what's happening. And we're building a system of systems, unified intelligence with a single pane of glass: one operational picture, one workflow language, one set of decision loops that can task the right asset for the right moment. And if you want persistence, the stratosphere holds the station. If you want precision, a UAV gets it done. If you want global context, space completes the frame. The operator shouldn't have to care which domain the data came from; they should care whether the right decision is made fastest and it's accountable. Let me close where I started. That mission: we invent the future of flight, we lift people up, and we bring them home safely. And for our defense business, that means the warfighter. A workhorse of our military fleet is the J85 engine. That engine powers the T-38 aircraft that the U.S. Air Force uses to train their next generation of pilots. This is an incredibly complex piece of hardware. It has over 6,000 individual piece parts that are managed across multiple different acquisition agencies. Keeping these engines available is critical to keeping planes in the air and pilots trained. This is a problem at the heart of U.S. national security and readiness. And at GE Aerospace, we feel that accountability deeply. And so a few years ago we decided to take a radical new approach to sustaining these engines, to combine the strength of our hardware with the power of software. So let's take you back in time, not just to 2024 when our journey with Palantir began, but all the way back to the 1960s. The integration of hardware and software has been a slow burn for over 60 years. In 1965, the Gemini spacecraft had the first-ever on-board computer in an aerospace application for their lunar mission. That advanced into the 1970s where we had fly-by-wire capability and computer-mediated flight controls. In the 80s that advanced to flight management systems, and in the 90s and 2000s we saw sensor integration and predictive maintenance capabilities. And with AI, the pace of software colliding with hardware is accelerating faster than we have ever seen it before. And GE Aerospace is uniquely positioned to capitalize on that convergence. As I shared, we have a legacy of over a century of hardware dominance, and the software maturity of a decade of production AI powering the most advanced fleet management capability in the world on our commercial fleet. So when solving the J85 problem, we knew we had the technical ability, but could we do it fast enough? Could we finally break through the structural barriers that have defined the defense industry for far too long? We needed a new approach, and so we partnered with Palantir to rebuild the operating system. So the J85 is where we began. We faced a 3x ramp in sustainment demand, getting more planes flying than we ever had before. And a single part of those 6,000 piece parts that make up that engine—a single bolt—can prevent a repair from getting a plane or getting an engine back on a plane and getting more pilots trained. And so to break these constraints, we started to build an ontology. We pulled in structured data, unstructured data, bills of materials, schematics, supplier data, technician notes, all coalesced into a single object model. This was intended to surface those problem parts. Which parts are going to prevent a repair and getting more pilots trained? But it wasn't just the parts that were going to cause a problem today; we were identifying parts that could cause a problem three, six, or twelve months down the road. And the thing that I love about this solution that we built is we weren't just providing visibility. This wasn't just a dashboard pointing people in the direction of the parts that were going to be constrained. Rather, it was recommending actions with the power of AI. Here is a problem, and here is how to solve it. So this quickly turned into a pilot with Palantir, the Defense Logistics Agency, and the U.S. Air Force, which is now under contract, focused on customer outcomes around fleet management and supply optimization. All built on that unified ontology. And for the first time, it wasn't taking months of back-and-forth painful communication to share information with our government customers. And operators finally had real visibility into exactly what was bottlenecking Air Force readiness and what actions they needed to take to improve that readiness posture. So in 2025, that focus evolved inward to expanding the ontology and with it, our impact on our customers. The expertise of our employees is an unrivaled asset and it is wasted on rote repetitive tasks. And so we empowered our people to identify and solve the right problems, putting their expertise to maximal use. In 2026, we are building a rich and powerful automation architecture with orchestration agents that are continuously monitoring and synthesizing signals, routing to functional expert agents across fulfillment, MRO, and customer service—all compounding into multiple workflows across the value chain. AI isn't replacing our expertise; it's amplifying it. It's freeing our people from the manual work of data wrangling, unlocking new degrees of freedom for our business, and enabling compounding outcomes for our customers. Here, agents detected a schedule delay from a material casing defect; it automatically generates a root-cause analysis and identifies the cost and schedule impacts. And because that ontology is connected across the entire program—employees, suppliers, inventory, variances—every recommendation is made with full operational context. So here the agent model is modeling that staffing reallocation option, trying to get around that schedule impact. It analyzes the situation, presents a solution, and it hands it off to a human. The human then reviews it, adjusts it as needed, and approves. And because of this, what used to take multiple phone calls, maybe emails and manual analysis can happen within minutes. And so the system then—once it's approved—the system executes, writes back to our systems, staffing schedules are updated, payroll's updated, and site supervisors are notified automatically. So this is an example of AI-human teaming where these agents aren't replacing our people; they're extending their capability, unlocking new capacities so then the experts can then put their attention on, you know, our most challenging problems. So here's another example of a quality failure of a bearing shipment. Without immediate action, this could cause weeks of delays, maybe a month's delay. In this case the agent's looking at cost versus schedule tradeoffs, analyzes alternatives, finds a solution, and it presents a recommendation. It recommends that we switch suppliers and fast-track a replacement order. Human then reviews and accepts; this cuts shipment time from three weeks to one week, reducing the delay and keeping us on the critical path. So what we saw was a defect detected, a root cause analyzed, staffing reallocated, and supplier rerouted—all automated, resolved, logged, and audible. Please welcome Chief Digital and Artificial Intelligence Officer from the US Department of War, Cameron Stanley. Good morning. I want to take you back to the year 2016 because that's really where the story of Project Maven began. Even before the project stood up formally as the Algorithmic Warfare Cross-Functional Team, because it began with a hypothesis: what does the third offset look like? If anyone knows about the offset strategies of the late 20th century, the first offset was basically the function of how do we overcome differences in mass and scale that our adversaries have on top of us in order to wield a more effective and aggressive military enterprise. What we saw from the first offset, which was how do you use nuclear weapons to overcome that, that was the first technology offset. Second one was stealth and precision-guided munitions. Secretary Carter, when he was trying to come up with the third offset, said the real advantage that we're going to have in the 21st century is decision advantage. How do you get better decisions faster than your adversary? That's what wins wars. The problem was in 2016, there were a lot of problems that we could look at for different types of technology. And AI was the one that he wanted to focus on, particularly for Project Maven. And so the Algorithmic Warfare Cross-Functional Team was established to basically say: get AI in the hands of the warfighter, focusing on UAV PED. What do I mean by that? What's happening for our airborne surveillance assets so that humans don't have to stare at screens? They get lazy, they get tired, they get distracted, they miss things. And so what we ran into was, let's use AI to try and tip and cue the right systems so that humans didn't just have to stare at a screen 16 hours a day and get tired. So with that, this is where we started. And the hypothesis was if we got AI in the hands of the warfighter, it would work. We developed some of the best computer vision models possible. They were built on our data, deployed on our systems, and everywhere in the world that we could possibly integrate it, we were integrated. You could detect cars and people; that was our primary challenge back in the global war on terror days. And so we actually fielded these systems quite robustly across the entire landscape. But the problem wasn't ISR PED per se. That was one problem. The bigger problem was the fact that our processes were not set up and our technology was not set up to use data-centric techniques in order to make better decisions faster. That just solved the problem of one individual in the entire chain. The real problem we were trying to solve was this. This is an image from a theater operations center in Scarlett Dragon. Scarlett Dragon is an exercise that Project Maven ran with the 18th Airborne Corps. And what you can see there is a bunch of static pictures, whiteboards. Don't be distracted by the screen that doesn't have any automated things on it; that's just PowerPoint slides. We literally were trying to push AI detections into workflows that humans were limiting on how our outputs and our outcomes that we were trying to achieve. And so we rejected the hypothesis that getting the AI in the hands of the warfighter was the right answer. What we really needed to do was take three steps back and say, the real issue isn't AI, the real issue is workflow. How are we making decisions? And that's when we came up with what I call the decision-centric approach. The decision-centric approach—I came up with nine questions. There are other approaches out there; this is my easy one, mainly because DARPA has their nine questions, I felt like I should have my nine. So when you're thinking about trying to improve decision-making processes, you always have to start with the decision. You also have to look at how the decision is made today. What part of the process are you accelerating? What data is required to make the decision? How is the data going to arrive? How will the user interact with that data? What's the reduction in human input? How are you measuring success and what's your iteration plan? So by looking at these, it's pretty straightforward. Those of us in the data world, we do this intuitively every single day. The challenge is getting senior leaders, especially those who are very competent and very professional, to understand that your job as a data professional is not to try and replace them. Your job is to make them better. And you make them better by giving them the data that they need in the time and the format and in the capacity that they require in order to make decisions. But there's another problem: that's one decision. And in complex workflows, as we know, there are dozens of decisions. The challenge isn't coming up with the right approach—we've got that. It's now how do we get the user community to buy into that approach in order to have them completely digitally transform their entire workflows? So that they can see how data-centric techniques allow them to solve all of those decisions simultaneously and get to real advantage, in my case on the battlefield. So what we did was—everybody in technology knows the flywheel quite well. Technology development, that's pretty straightforward, right? Standard spiral development process; no one should be surprised by that. What we fail to recognize usually in the technology development space, at least in the department, is that there's a process improvement flywheel that's happening at the same time, ideally. The question isn't how are you improving the process with the technology; it's a question of how do you interlink and couple those two flywheels together? So that you're delivering technology at the right phase in their process and they're giving you the operational feedback in the right phase of your process so that you can have mutual, synergistic improvements in how we approach things. It's not just the technology; it's also the process. The process is clear; that's what we need to be focusing on. And what we found when we looked at the AI into different types of workflows is that the system was wrong. This is Maven Smart System, Palantir's software-as-a-service product that we are deploying across the entire department. As you can see, it's not just one data feed; it's multiple. And instead of having eight or nine systems for those decision-makers to look at every single day in order for them to make decisions, you then fuse it into a single visualization tool. The single visualization tool allows you to select and de-select different types of data and look at different approaches to data. But more importantly, action from the same system that you're trying to develop your workflows around. Once you have a detection that you want to actually move into a targeting workflow, for example, this is what we do. Left-click, right-click, left-click—magically it becomes a detection. That detection then gets moved into a workflow. This is standard digitized workflow, but I want to walk you through it quickly. You have different types of targets that are identified on the left there. Every single column produces a different type of decision-making process. Once you have that decision and you're trying to actually action that process, we now move into COA generation—course of action generation—where we are automatically via a number of factors trying to identify what the best asset to prosecute a target looks like. Once we've got the different approaches and we select one, we then can move directly into how do we action that target. So we've gone from identifying the target to now coming up with a course of action to now actioning that target, all from one system. This is revolutionary. We were having this done in about eight or nine systems where humans were literally moving detections left and right in order to get to our desired end state, in this case actually closing a kill chain. When we started this, it literally took hours to do what you just saw there. And through a number of different deployments, we've been able to reduce that time significantly. All because of two things: one, our ability to actually integrate with our customer base directly and have them work with us in order to understand their process and us developing the right technology. But more importantly, connect those disparate systems in a way that's never been done before, using an abstraction layer called Maven Smart System that connects and interconnects all of those things with the right data approach, the right data ontology, and the right data formatting to connect these systems. This did not happen overnight. This took seven years to get here—not only from a data connections perspective, but also to connect each of those systems together. So where are we going from here? Obviously, there's many things that we have to accomplish. We're not done yet. And this is the thing that gets me up in the morning as the CDIO of the department: every single tool that I get from my vendors, Palantir especially, updates with time. It's the first time in my career that I have a system that gets better day after day after day because we are integrated with our customers, we are listening to their feedback, and we're giving them the tools that they ask for—not what some requirements manager asked them to build five years ago. It's literally happening today. And I do this every single day because I care about one thing and one thing only: that 18, 19, or 20-year-old kid who had no choice in where he went or what threat he's facing, because I want him to win and come home. Palantir is very helpful in delivering this. Maven Smart System is an incredible system. And, yeah, I live my life: no fair fights. If I can avoid it, let's not have fair fights. I'm going to hit three concrete things very quickly. First, what I think has changed. Two, why I think that is so important when we think about how we organize together. As examples of that, we had a team that did a factory tour last week at a battery pack manufacturer. They—we love to do the actual concrete—see how the thing actually works. And they recorded the factory tour, understanding how the factory actually worked. They had a discursive discussion about what works well, what breaks, what happens when the product leaves the factory, and what are the types of constraints that are imposed on the factory that make their job difficult. They took that recording, fed it into AIP, integrated it with the technology and the tradecraft that is being coded in AIP, and then the team over a 24-hour window was able to develop four applications. A customer-facing application, an operator-facing application to manage the line, a technician assistant to be able to increase uptime, and a corporate quality workflow across plants that are addressing upstream issues around design, engineering, procurement, and quality. And they were able to deliver those four applications in a 24-hour window. Second, we had another deployment that was on-site with a global logistics provider and while on-site, cartel violence broke out in Mexico. This completely disrupted their operation; they had to reroute a lot of different things. Through the course of the afternoon, the team built a global disruption manager to track what was happening in Mexico, but also extended it for all of the different geographies that they operate in. And then critically, take that and link it into as they observed what do you actually do when one of these disruptions happen: how do I manage product rerouting, material holds, and changing the quoting process in terms of influencing what's actually hitting the logistics network? They were able to do that in an afternoon to now have something that is available for the next disruption, like a disruption in the Strait of Hormuz. At a brand new customer where we were just starting, a customer that manages a very large fleet, the initial challenge statement was: can you build a better predictive model to manage fleet availability? And in the first 24 hours the team was able to build four models that outperformed the existing state-of-the-art. In the next 24 hours they built agents that were able to consistently monitor all the inputs that were describing the actual state of the network and challenge assumptions that the models were built on in order to fix those forecasts live. The next day they integrated that with inventory data and sales forecasting and an optimization engine. And by the fourth day, had an automated routing engine that was suggesting ways to reallocate tens of thousands of assets for maximum throughput and quality. That happened in four days. Lest you think that this type of pacing is only happening in clean, pristine, new environments, you already heard from SAP and Accenture today about how this technology is allowing you to accelerate the rate at which you can get to the technology you want to run by migrating away from the old technology that you have. We're seeing that across legacy systems everywhere. Louis Gave: $120 Oil Breaks Everything — And Nobody Is Ready

The Julia La Roche Show(67.3K) ·57:55 ·7.7K views · 2026-03-12
Louis-Vincent Gave (Founder and CEO, Gavekal Research) Julia La Roche (Host, The Julia La Roche Show)
Louis Gave argues that the global economy has entered a structurally inflationary regime where energy prices serve as the primary risk and essential equity hedge, rendering the traditional 60/40 portfolio obsolete.
TL;DR Louis Gave argues that the global economy has entered a structurally inflationary regime where energy prices serve as the primary risk and essential equity hedge, rendering the traditional 60/40 portfolio obsolete. While the U.S. remains energy-resilient, Gave warns that...

TL;DR

Louis Gave argues that the global economy has entered a structurally inflationary regime where energy prices serve as the primary risk and essential equity hedge, rendering the traditional 60/40 portfolio obsolete. While the U.S. remains energy-resilient, Gave warns that sustained oil prices above $120/barrel will "break" global allies and highlights a massive investment opportunity in the "stupidly undervalued" Chinese Renminbi.

The Three Prices Driving the Macro Framework

  • The Critical Pillars: Gave’s investment process is dictated by three primary prices: the U.S. Dollar, the 10-year U.S. Treasury yield, and the price of energy. Getting the direction of these three right accounts for 80-90% of investment success.
  • The Bonds-War Correlation: Following the insight of Luke Gromen, Gave notes that "bonds are the second casualty of war." In a conflict scenario, bonds suffer because wars are inherently inflationary events.
  • The Binary Iran Outcome:
      • Scenario A (Prolonged Conflict): Continued instability in the Straits of Hormuz leads to supply chain dislocations and an inflationary hit to the economy, which is disastrous for bonds.
      • Scenario B (Regime Change): While a "Berlin Wall" moment in Iran would be long-term bullish, the massive demand for capital to rebuild the Middle East would likely drive real interest rates significantly higher, as seen during German reunification.

Energy as the New Portfolio Hedge

  • The Death of 60/40: The traditional 60% equity/40% bond portfolio thrived in a deflationary era. In today’s structurally inflationary world, bonds no longer provide protection against geopolitical or supply-side shocks.
  • The 60/20/20 Rule: Gave recommends a new allocation: 60% Equities, 20% Precious Metals, and 20% Energy.
      • Energy as Protection: In an inflationary bust, energy is the only asset that hedges against the primary risk—a spike in energy costs that collapses growth.
  • Oil Price Thresholds:
      • $120/barrel: This is the "break point" where high costs begin to crush U.S. consumption and global growth.
      • Market Mispricing: Current futures markets suggest the oil spike is temporary. Gave disagrees, fearing the upside risk is more consequential and lasting than the market currently prices in.

Geopolitical Resilience: U.S. vs. Global Allies

  • U.S. Self-Sufficiency: Because the U.S. is energy self-sufficient, $100+ oil is essentially a "right pocket, left pocket" transfer within the domestic economy (from consumers to energy-producing states like Texas and North Dakota).
  • The Fragility of Allies: High oil prices are catastrophic for Europe and East Asia.
      • Supply Dependency: 90% of oil flowing through the Straits of Hormuz goes East to China, India, Japan, and Korea.
      • Natural Gas Crisis: Taiwan has only roughly 10 days of natural gas reserves; any prolonged blockage of trade routes forces a shift back to coal and creates a massive "global growth shock."
  • The Weaponization of Treasuries: Since the seizure of Russian assets, central banks (China, Saudi Arabia, etc.) no longer view U.S. Treasuries as risk-free. This has fueled a parabolic run in gold as central banks diversify away from Western sovereign debt.

The China Misconception and the Trade War "Win"

  • The Trade War Outcome: Gave asserts that China won the trade war, albeit at a high domestic cost.
      • Evidence: China’s trade surplus has grown from $20 billion/month to $100 billion/month since the conflict began.
      • De-Westernization: China successfully spent the last eight years de-Westernizing its supply chains, making it resilient to U.S. embargoes in critical sectors like semiconductors and AI.
  • The "Hunger Games" of Capitalism: Contrary to the "central planning" narrative, China's growth is driven by hyper-competition. For example, local government incentives created 100 electric car producers, of which only a few survive, resulting in world-class EVs priced under $10,000.
  • The Renminbi Opportunity:
      • Undervaluation: The Renminbi is the most undervalued asset in the world due to massive productivity gains and domestic deflation that have not been priced into the currency.
      • Investment Case: With zero inflation, China has the most room for fiscal and monetary easing compared to the West.
"Economic activity is energy transformed... The lower an energy price, the easier it is for entrepreneurs to transform energy into corporate profits and into economic growth." — Louis Gave
"If truth is the first casualty of war, bonds are usually a close second." — Louis Gave (quoting Luke Gromen)
"The Renminbi is just stupidly stupidly undervalued... You can't understand how cheap the Renminbi is until you go visit China yourself... This is the obvious trade of the coming years." — Louis Gave

I had not heard this idea and I really am fascinated by it. Explain that and when did you kind of come to that realization that that's the hedge? In our process, so there's several starting points. I mentioned the three prices. Another big part of our process is acknowledging that asset prices are driven by the interaction of economic activity and inflation. So if you draw four quadrants, you have an up a horizontal line that is more or less inflation and a vertical line that is more or less economic growth. That gives you four possible investment scenarios. A deflationary bust, which is essentially what Japan had through the 1990s and 2000s. A deflationary boom, which is the the default mode of capitalism. The deflationary boom is the default mode of capitalism because every entrepreneur, every CEO wakes up in the morning thinking, how do I do more with less? How can I produce more whatever widget I make or more services with fewer workers, with less energy, with less copper, whatever the inputs are, you're always trying to produce more with less and so that's why capitalism is normally a deflationary boom. At the same time, I think the natural state of most democracies is the inflationary boom. Because most politicians, if the entrepreneur says how do I produce more with less, the the politician says how can I promise more to my voters without having to pay for it? And the the answer is by leaning on central banks to print more money and by running big budget deficits. So the the default mode of most democracies is the inflationary boom. And then every now and then you get an inflationary bust, which is when growth— it's also known as stagflation— growth goes down, inflation goes up, and that almost always happens because energy prices spike. For most of my career, and I'm 52 years old, for most of my career, we lived in a broadly deflationary environment. We lived in a broadly deflationary environment because again that's the default mode of capitalism. We lived in a broadly deflationary environment because Japan had an epic bust. We broadly lived in a broadly deflationary environment because China was a a huge deflationary force in and of itself. And to some extent so was technology. Now, in this environment, that meant that each time you got a geopolitical shock or some kind of exogenous shock, because the overall backdrop was deflationary, central bank and fiscal authorities could step in and push money into the system. They they had very little constraint to to their to their policies. And you saw this time and time again. You saw this up to an extreme of Covid. Covid hit, ultimate exogenous shock. Thing comes out of nowhere. It's nobody had this in their model. Economies go in lockdown, economic growth collapses. The response of the policy makers is to embark on a fiscal binge and a monetary policy easing binge such as the world had never seen. And the end result of this, we pushed so much liquidity into the system that we actually broke the structural deflationary world in which we lived in and moved into a more structurally inflationary world. Now, once you've essentially moved from the bottom when you're in the bottom of the quadrant and you're always wondering am I in a deflationary boom or deflationary bust? Again the hedge you buy equities and you hedge with bonds. That's why through these 30 years the best possible portfolio was 60% equities 40% bonds. Whenever you moved to the deflationary bust, your bonds saved the day. We now live in a structurally inflationary world for a host of reasons that I go through in in my 2021 book. And in a structurally inflationary world, the 60/40 no longer works. What you need to do is you move to a portfolio that's still 60% equities, but you forget the 40% bonds, you buy 20% precious metals and 20% energy. Because the risk is always that you get an energy price spike that pushes you into the inflationary bust quadrant. And so there you go. Now people don't your listeners don't need to buy the book. Gold is a hedge against bad monetary policy and essentially 0% interest rates. And I think you've seen this again in the past few years where gold has gone absolutely parabolic. What's been fascinating as gold has gone parabolic is who the buyers of gold have been. On the one hand it's been central banks. And that's a geopolitical shift. When when the western world seized Russia's assets and told Russia, "Look you get access to your US Treasuries, your German bunds, your UK gilts if we say you do and if you don't act like a total jerk," it sent a very strong message to the central banks of China, of Indonesia, of Saudi Arabia, of Qatar, of everybody that the the US Treasuries were no longer a risk-free asset. And that in a conflict you could easily lose access to them. And so central banks that had been net sellers of gold up until that point became net buyers of gold and if you look at the past three years, central banks have have bought 25% of the outstanding gold mine production every year. So that's been a huge shift in the gold price. But the other massive gold buyers have been the Japanese savers, the Chinese savers, the Korean savers. And why have these guys been buying gold? First because their currencies were weakening and second because they had 0% interest rates at home. And so Chile comes to mind, Brazil comes to mind, China, South Africa, I think all of these markets are super exciting. Okay, back to China. Do you live in Hong Kong or you mostly there? I do. Okay. What's the biggest misconception around China that you hear specifically maybe looking from like— I know you're French, you're European, but I'm talking about like American investors. Like maybe most of the folks that would watch this show, what do you think is the biggest misconception on China? Boy, how much time do you have? Oh, we have we have another 20 minutes Louis. I don't know if you want to put a coin in that machine. Look, I think the the biggest misconception of all is when you say look China's doing well and you know they're they're growing very fast and they've taken 800 million people out of poverty over the the past couple of generations etc. It makes people in America feel very uncomfortable. And they immediately say so oh so does that mean you believe in central planning? Do you believe in communism? Etc. And I think the the biggest misconception when it comes to China is people fail to understand where the source and the genesis of of China's growth story really comes from. And the story of China's economic growth miracle, because it is a miracle, you've never had another country grow so far, so fast, pull 800 million people out of abject poverty. Let's not forget that when I was growing up I was told finish your plate because there's little children in China that are starving. And and that was true, my parents weren't lying to me. I never really quite got the logic of why I needed to finish my plate and the Chinese kids starving, but to see how far it's come in a couple generations is is really mind blowing. So how did it happen? It wasn't the wonders of central planning. It really wasn't. The first thing that happened is that Mao died. And Mao was to a large extent a control freak. He needed to control absolutely everything. That went down to what people wore to work, the uniforms they had to wear to work, and where people ate their lunch and where people ate their dinner. So much so that when Deng Xiaoping came to power following Mao, he inherited total control, not power, total control over the country. But he realized that fundamentally he had no power. Because economically China was a midget and really had no role to play in the global economy. China was a fifth of global population and wasn't even 5% of global GDP. So he started something. He said well look, we're going to start liberalizing the labor markets and see where that takes us. So he gave up some control and the economy took off. And he realized that by giving up control you could get more power. And so he liberalized the labor market and the first fortunes in China were made by guys who built factories there to exploit the the local labor. And then in the late 90s you had the Asian crisis, you hit a big economic bump. And the Chinese leadership liberalized real estate. All of a sudden you could own your your real estate. And that unleashed an epic boom in China, an epic real estate boom that probably went overboard. But they again gave up control, i.e. control over real estate, for more for more growth and for more power. They liberalized also the commodity space. All of a sudden you could be a coal mine operator, copper and all these things, an oil trader and build your own refinery. So they liberalized the commodity space and again that was another boom. So having liberalized commodities, having liberalized land, having liberalized labor, you now are at the final frontier where you have to liberalize the world of capital. And here the leadership is obviously very worried about it because they've seen how liberalizing capital can lead to massive accidents. They were starting the process of liberalizing the world of capital and the 2008 crisis showed up and and it scared the hell out of them. They said okay, hold on, if we liberalize capital does that mean that all of a sudden we're going to blow up our banking system like the US just blew up theirs? Because if we do that that has massive massive negative implications for our overall economy. So the past 10, 15 years has this has been this back and forth of liberalization on the capital front. So I think that's the first big big misconceptions. I think the other massive misconception that that westerners have about China is they believe that essentially you have these national champions that the government picks national champions, subsidizes them, and essentially then these guys become global champions. It actually doesn't work that way. The way the system works in China is that Xi Jinping, the guy at the very top will say, "You know what guys, we need to produce electric cars." And he'll say that to all the mayors and the provincial governors and the party secretaries of different regions. And then all these guys go back to their district thinking, "Well, if I want to get the next job, I have to be the best producer of electric cars." And I can do this in different ways. So I can either cut a deal with Tesla and get them to come and build electric cars in my district, which incidentally is what the mayor of Shanghai did and in time he's now the premier, he's now the number two in the Chinese government. Or I lean on my local bank to make loans to to my local producer who will then start producing electric cars. And before you know it you have 100 car producers in China because you have 100 different mayors who are leaning on 100 different banks to produce local cars. And then it really becomes the Hunger Games of capitalism. Out of those 100, five will survive. And the end result is great for the consumer who gets much cheaper cars and and massive competition. So it's actually funny because it's it's pretty much the opposite of what happens in the western world. In the western world we say okay, we want to do electric cars, so let's give a bunch of subsidies to Tesla, let's make sure that Elon Musk becomes a trillionaire, and you end up with one electric car company i.e. Tesla that produces quite expensive cars. In China you end up with a 100 producers of cars and now you can get pretty sweet electric cars in China for less than 10,000 bucks a car. And now they're selling them all around the world. China five years ago wasn't exporting any cars and now China's the biggest car exporter in the world. So the level of again, the way you have to conceptualize Chinese capitalism is that it is the Hunger Games of capitalism where people survive surviving in that environment is extremely extremely challenging, extremely tough. How do you think China is looking at the war in Iran? I think they think of it first and foremost in economic terms. Not so much in geopolitical terms, but first and foremost in economic terms. Iran was a great deal for China because they got to buy a lot of oil for very cheap. They got to buy a lot of oil at big discounts. And this allowed a lot of tin-pot refineries up and down China's coast to keep going thanks to thanks to cheap Iranian crude. To the extent that right now this is obviously undercut, this is not good economic news for China, it's bad news for all those tin-pot refineries. So I think that's how China primarily thinks about this. It is, "Well this is a shame, we used to get cheap crude now now we're not going to get it." Now China's relationship with the broader Middle East is first and foremost one where they look at that part of the world as energy suppliers. And what China wants out of that part of the world is stability for its from its energy. So I think in the US you read a lot of articles in the media, "Oh how come China is not doing more to help Iran," etc., "China's a fair-weather friend" and all that. But I don't think there was ever any illusion that Iran is an ally of China or anything of the sort. For China the relationship it has with the US, both economically but also increasingly politically, is far more important than any relationship it has with Iran. So it's not going to go out of its way to help promote a war, help continue this thing. I think China just wants to get back to business, wants to get back to getting cheap oil. And if you get regime change in Iran and the regime continues to turn around and say— the regime will have no really no choice but to sell oil to China anyway. So I think if you're Xi Jinping, you're not so much bummed that, "Oh my god the the US is bombing my friend Khamenei like this is terrible," as much as "Oh darn like am I going to lose my cheap oil here." Hmm, yeah. Okay. What do you think right now is the most underappreciated macro theme? What would that be for you? To be honest, I going back to the idea of the three prices, I think the oil is wrongly priced right now especially at the long end. I think people are underestimating the upside potential on oil and how quickly this this might move. Now we've seen a lot of commodity prices shoot up in the past 18 months. We've seen gold shoot up, we've seen silver shoot up, we've seen copper shoot up. None of that has a big macro impact. You and I don't need to buy gold, right? I mean it's nice if we get it if prices— I like my gold. I know sure you do, I'm sure you do. And I need to buy gold for Valentine's Day and for my wife's birthday. I get that. But economically it's not devastating if that doesn't happen. Not in the same way that energy prices have a direct economic impact and if they start mooning like like all the other commodities did frankly in the past 18 months, we enter into a very different world very quickly. So I think that's one thing that's underappreciated, underestimated. And then if I look around the world and I think okay what price out there feels completely wrong? Like what price of all the things all the markets etc., what is the price that is just completely stupid and really is too far gone from reality and from where it should be? For me the obvious one is the Chinese exchange rate, the Renminbi. The Renminbi is just stupidly stupidly undervalued. And I think for a number of reasons, which I'm happy to go into if you want, but that undervaluation is starting to be taken in. The Renminbi's gone up seven months in a row now. Even in this period of uncertainty over the past 10 days that we've had the Renminbi hasn't really been been smoked or anything. And I tell this to everybody I meet, you can't understand how cheap the Renminbi is until you go visit China yourself. Because essentially the Renminbi is at the same level as it's been— a little down, but for the past five years. But over the past five years, the productivity gains that you've had in China and the inherent deflation you've had in China while at the same time you've had massive inflation across the western world— prices in the United States are up 35% from where they were five years ago, and that's on official data. In China they're flat, but I think they're actually down 10 or 20% from where they were five years ago. So the currency should have adjusted for this massive shift in prices and they haven't. So today you can go, if you don't believe me go to Expedia and go book a five-star hotel room in Shanghai or in Beijing. Oh I've been I've been to Shanghai, yeah in 2017. Well okay, so the hotel you stayed at in Shanghai in 2017? I stayed in the Shangri-La, yeah. Well it's probably half the price it was back then. Meanwhile the the same hotel in New York will have gone double. You can stay in literally world-class hotels in Shanghai and Beijing for 100 bucks a 100 bucks a night. And you can have some of the best meals you've ever had for 20 bucks. The cab to the airport is less than 10 bucks, so on and so forth. So the price of the Renminbi is now so wrong that for me this is the obvious trade of the coming years. And so again you look at the fundamentals, China's got tremendous productivity gains etc., all of which push towards a higher currency. You look at the momentum, it's now moving, seven months in a row. You look at the investor positioning, absolutely nobody owns Renminbi. And then you look at the valuations and it's the most extreme case of undervaluation of any asset I can think of in the world. So if you think of slam dunk trades, of trades you can put on with a lot of confidence, for me that's gotta be one of them. Was that intentional though for them almost to keep it like weaker? But why? Like what's the benefit? So what happened— okay, you know the old Steve Martin quote, right? That before you criticize anyone you have to walk a mile in their shoes. When you criticize them you're a mile away and you have their shoes. So if you look at China in 2018, the US weaponized the semiconductor supply chain against China. They they said nobody's allowed to sell high-end semiconductors to China anymore. The leadership— the Chinese leadership— panicked at that moment. They thought, "Okay if they can block us from semiconductors they can block us from anything, they can block us from chemical products, they can block us from auto parts." We need to be self-sufficient in literally everything. And so we're going to we're going to capture all of our savings which happen to be massive and instead of those savings going into equities or real estate like they used to, we're going to take all that money and we're going to push them down and essentially build up our own industry. And that's exactly what China did. That's why real estate prices went down a third, that's why equity prices went down two-thirds. It was a weaponization of China's savings towards one very clear goal which was de-westernizing all of its supply chains. Now as you try to do that, as you try to ramp up your own industry, one of the simplest ways to ramp up your own industry is to keep an undervalued exchange rate. It is to make sure that your your local industry won't fear the competition of Japan, of Korea, of the US, of Europe, etc. And so for eight years the Renminbi was kept artificially low as the as China felt we need to cushion ourselves against further US embargoes, against further US attacks. This all ended last year when Trump comes back to power, President Trump comes back to power and decides to this time not just punch China in the face but punch absolutely everybody. He turns around and punches Europe, he punches Canada, he punches Mexico, he punches India. And alone amongst all the countries, because China got punched eight years ago and eight years ago China couldn't fight back. They were too worried that the US would embargo everything. But they didn't sit on their hands either. They went to the gym, they got fit, they got strong, they de-westernized their supply chain so that when Trump comes back in and punches them on the nose again in 25, China stands up and says, "Okay you want to go, let's go. You tariff me, I tariff you. You embargo me, I embargo you." And the US then has to fold. When the CEO of Raytheon says, "Hey, in three weeks we can't produce missiles anymore," or when the CEO of Ford says, "In two weeks we gotta shut down our plants because we don't have the rare earths and we don't have the magnets," China had achieved what they needed to achieve. So now they no longer have to worry about potential embargoes; they no longer have to worry about not getting supplies of something, about supply chain dislocations, etc. They now produce everything they need domestically. I think DeepSeek showed that even at the very pinnacle of technology, i.e. AI, they had enough chips to to go ahead and do things. Perhaps they are not quite as good as the US, but they are close enough and competitive. And so this has been the massive shift that has gone on in the Chinese economy, and this is why now they no longer need to mobilize all their savings to push industry. They can now allow the stock market to go up, they can allow the real estate market to come back up, and they can also allow their currency to to come back up. And this is what's unfolding now. Who, who won the trade war? Well, who raised the white flag? I don't want to make like a Jesuit answering a question with another question, but who said, "Okay, fine, we're done"? So I would say that China won the trade war, but it won it at tremendous cost to itself. It is a little bit like how the Soviet Union won the Second World War, but it won it at immense cost—at a cost that most countries wouldn't even contemplate. The Soviet Union took 20 million soldiers' losses, etc. It took on a huge, huge human toll to win the Second World War. China took on a huge economic toll to to win the trade war, but now it's won it. And so now, on the other side of it, you get the stronger Renminbi, and you get the outperforming Chinese equity market. While the trade war was going on, the Renminbi was going down and Chinese equities were underperforming. As it became obvious to anyone looking that actually China has now won the trade war—China, let me put it to you this way. When the trade war started, China was running a 20 billion a month trade surplus. Eight years into the trade war, China's trade surplus is now 100 billion a month. China's trade surplus is now bigger than Saudi Arabia's GDP. So if you're Donald Trump and you think of trade wars in mercantilist terms, and your scoring card is the trade surplus, it's pretty clear who's won. So, I have four teenagers, so I, I don't sleep soundly at all given with with my four teenagers. It's happy days; everything is fine. Most entrepreneurs and most business people can transform energy into economic growth and into profits decently. It's always when energy prices shoot up that things start derailing. So, so yeah, I do worry about energy. That's why I'm loaded up with energy in my own portfolio and in the portfolios we manage, because for me, that's the big risk. And if energy prices don't go up, then the rest of the stuff will make money and it'll be happy days. Yeah. Is there a price level that it'll be like, "Oh boy, here we go," like you'll know? The Most Founder Mode CEO Working Today Isn’t the Founder: Opendoor’s Kaz Nejatian

Sequoia Capital(132.0K) ·62:51 ·3.8K views · 2026-03-12
Kaz Nejatian (CEO, Opendoor)
Kaz Nejatian discusses his "refounding" of Opendoor, arguing that enduring companies are built on the "first derivatives" of their core business and that true "Founder Mode" requires a radical prioritization of outcomes over processes and a total rejection of life’s defaults.
TL;DR Kaz Nejatian discusses his "refounding" of Opendoor, arguing that enduring companies are built on the "first derivatives" of their core business and that true "Founder Mode" requires a radical prioritization of outcomes over processes and a total rejection of life’s...

TL;DR

Kaz Nejatian discusses his "refounding" of Opendoor, arguing that enduring companies are built on the "first derivatives" of their core business and that true "Founder Mode" requires a radical prioritization of outcomes over processes and a total rejection of life’s defaults.

The Theory of "First Derivative" Businesses

  • Core Insight: Kaz argues that the most successful companies in history are built on the first derivative of their primary business rather than the core activity itself.
      • Historical Examples: Union Pacific made more money from land and homes than the railway; Google search is the core, but ads are the high-value first derivative.
      • Platform Evolution: Shopify evolved from a SaaS tool into a merchant services powerhouse (payments, tax, billing) because those derivatives provide the long-term enduring value that society often misunderstands.
  • Truth Over Sunk Costs: Kaz details Shopify’s decision to divest from logistics.
      • Adaptive Strategy: The decision took only three weeks because the "facts on the ground" changed; once third-party options like Walmart and DHL became viable, Shopify didn't need to own concrete to serve its merchants.
      • The "Main Mission": Leaders must be willing to abandon "side quests" even if they were major investments if those projects no longer serve the core truth of the business.

Leadership and "Founder Mode" for Non-Founders

  • Outcomes vs. Processes: Kaz defines the difference between professional managers and founders by their focus.
      • Process vs. Truth: Professional managers often prioritize process and appearance because their power comes from management; founders (and founder-mode CEOs) prioritize truth and outcome delivery.
      • The "Founder Seat": A company doesn't necessarily need its original founder, but it must have someone occupying the "founder seat" who exerts "exothermic pressure" on the system.
  • Strong Attract, Strong Repel: Upon taking over Opendoor, Kaz refreshed almost the entire executive team within two quarters.
      • The User Manual: Kaz uses a decade-old "Blueprint" (a user manual for working with him) to help employees opt-in or opt-out immediately.
      • Trust Battery: Borrowing from Shopify CEO Tobi Lütke, Kaz starts with high trust (75%) but allows it to deplete rapidly if performance or alignment fails.

Overriding Life and Career Defaults

  • Deliberate Radicalism: Kaz believes you cannot "half-ass" a change in direction; you must override defaults with full force.
      • Personal Defaults: Kaz identifies as a religious tech CEO who reads the Bible daily and goes to church, a choice he made deliberately to stay grounded and avoid the "rat race" of constant comparison.
      • Stewardship vs. Status: He advises optimizing for *doing* things (stewardship) rather than *being* things (status). Those who optimize for service often find happiness as a byproduct, whereas those who optimize for happiness directly often fail.
  • Time Horizons: He views "quarters" as useless metrics.
      • The Weekly/Decadal Split: Effective leaders should focus on "this week" (tactical execution/validation) and "ten years from now" (long-term vision).

The Mechanics of the Opendoor Turnaround

  • Incentive Alignment: Kaz rejected a standard executive pay package, choosing a $1 salary and performance-based units (PSUs) that have zero value unless the stock performs.
      • Against RSUs: He argues that standard RSUs incentivize professional managers to "manage a slow decline" rather than take the risks necessary for a turnaround.
  • AI-Native Transformation: To shift Opendoor into an AI company, Kaz made "defaulting to AI" a primary metric in performance reviews.
      • Practical Application: In a recent company hackathon, a general contractor with no coding experience used tools like Cursor and OpenAI to automate his entire job, transitioning from a manual worker to a manager of software.

Personal Integration and Resilience

  • Work-Life Integration: Kaz rejects the standard notion of work-life balance, instead aligning his entire family (wife and children) around the company’s mission.
      • The Family Mission: His children understand Opendoor’s product, and he credits his family for providing the resilience needed to survive the "worst days," such as large-scale layoffs.
  • The "Career" Mindset: He distinguishes between a "job" (doing something for pay) and a "career" (something you work on for yourself).
      • Craftsmanship: He advises young professionals to "work all the hours" to find their breaking points and master their craft, noting that the health and wealth benefits of marriage and family are statistically significant for long-term CEO success.
"Companies are exoskeletons of their leaders if they're good... The question is not whether the founder is there or not; the question is whether the founder seat is taken or not." — Kaz Nejatian
"Founder mode means taking responsibility for outcomes, not processes. Professional managers care more about process than truth. They care more about what things look like than what they are." — Kaz Nejatian
"Watching CNBC does not make you a good professional CEO, it just doesn't. I don't genuinely understand CEOs that have the stock price on a Bloomberg terminal in their office." — Kaz Nejatian
